1.2 WATER QUALITY PROBLEWS

Wator used for irrigation can vary greatly In quality depending
upon type and quantity of dissolved salts. Salts are present in irri:
gation Water in relatively small but significant amounts. They origi-
fate” from Glssolution or weathering of the rocks and soil, including
Gissolution of lime, Gypsum and other slowly dissolved soil minerals:
These salts are carried with the water to wherovor it is used: In the
case’ of irrigation, the salts are applied with the water and remain
behind in the Soil a5 water evaporates or is used by the crop.

The suitability of a water for irrigation is determined not only
by the total amount of salt present but also by the King of salt.
Various soil and cropping problems develop as the total salt content
increases, and special management practices may be required to maintain
acceptable crop yields. Wator quality or Suitability for use ie Judged
Gn the potential severity of problems that can be expected to develop
uring long-corn use.

The problems that result vary both in kind and degree, and are
modified by Soil, climate and crop, fs well as by the Skill and
knowledge Of ‘the Water user, As a result, there is ho Set limit on
water quality rather, ite Suitability for use is determined by tho
Conditions of use which affect the accumulation of the water can
stituents and wnich may restrict crop yield. Tha soil problems most
Commonly encountered and used as a basis to evaluate water quality are
Chose related to salinity, water infiltration rate. toxicity and a
Group of other miscellaneous problems.

1.2.0 saliniey

A salinity problem exists if salt accumulates in the crop root
zone to a concentration that causes a loss in yield. In irrigated
reas, these salts often originate from a saline, high water table or
Ero salts in the applied water. Yield reductions occur when the salts
accumulate in the root ome to such an extent that the crop is mo
Longer able to extract sufficient water from the salty Soil solution,
resulting in a water stress for a significant period of time. If water
Uptake 15 appreciably reduced, the plant slows its rate of growth. The
plant symptoms are similar in appearance to those of Grought, such ar
Wilting, or a darker, blulsh-green colour and Sometimes thicker, waxier
Teaves: Symptoms vary With the growth stage, being more noticeable if
the salts’ affect the plant during the early stages of grosth. In some
Cases, mild salt effects may go entirely unnoticed because of a uniform
reduction in Growth across an entire field.

Salts that contribute to a salinity problem are water soluble
ana readily transported by water. A portion ‘of the salts thar accumu
Tate trom rior irrigations can be moved (leached) below the rooting
Gepth Lf more irrigation water infiltrates the Soil than is used by tne
Crop during the crop season. Leaching is the key to controlling a water
Quality related salinity problem. Over a period of Lime, salt removal
By” leaching must equal or exceed the sali additions from the sppiied
water "to prevent salt builaing up to o damaging concentration. The
amount “of "leaching required is dependent upon the irrigation water
Guality and the salinity tolerance of the crop grown:

Salt content of the root zone varies with depth. It varies from
approximately that of the irrigation vater near the soil Surface to
many times that of the applied water at the bottom of the rooting
Gepth. Salt concentration increases with depth dus to plants extracting
ater but leaving salts benind in a greatly reduces volume of soil
ater. Fach subsequent irrigation pusies (leaches) the salts deeper
Theo the root zone where they continue to accumulate until leached. The
Lower rooting ‘Gepth salinity will depend upon the leaching that has
occurred.

Following an irrigation, the most readily available water is in
the upper root fone ~ 4 low salinity area. An the crop uses water, the
upper root zone becomes depleted and the zone Of most readily available
water changes toward the Jesper parts as the time interval between
Trrigacions is extended. Thesa lower depths are usually more salty. Fhe
crop. does not respond to the extremes of low or high salinity in the
Tooting depth but integrates ater availability and takes water from
Wherever It is most readily available. Irrigation timing is thus
Important in maintaining o Nigh Soil-water availability and reducing
che problems caused when fhe Crop must draw a Significant portion of
its water from the less available, higher salinity soil-water deeper in
the root zone. For good crop production, equal importance must be given
To maintaining a high foilowater availanility an to leaching accumu
lated salts from the rooting depth before the salt concentration
exceeds the tolerance af the plants

For crops irrigated infrequently, as is normal when using
surface methods and conventional IFFigation management, crop yield is
Beit ‘Correlated with the avorsge root zone Salinity, but for crops
{Frigated on a daily, or near daily basi (localized or rip irriga-
hia rep Yid1on ave necter correlates wit the water-uptake weighted
£oot zone salinity (shoades 1982). The differences are not great but
may become important in the higher range of salinity. Tn tnis paper,
Hiscussions are based on crop response to the average root zone
Saline.

--- PÁGINA 18 ---
“ae

In irrigated agriculture, many salinity problems are associated
with or strongly influenced by a shallow water table (within 2 metres
Gf the surface). Salts accumulate in this water table and frequently
become an important additional source of salt that moves upward inte
the crop root zone. Control Of an existing Ahallou water table is thus
essential to salinity control and to successful long-ters irrigated
agriculture. HiGhor salinity water requires appreciable extra water for
Leaching, which adds greatly to a potential water table (drainage
problem and makes long-term irrigated agriculture nearly impossible co
Achieve Vitnout adequate drainage. If drainage 1s adequate, salinity
Control becomes Simply good management to snsure that the crop is
adequately supplied With water at all times and that enough leaching
water is applied to control salts within the tolerance of the crop.
1.2.2 water Infiltration Rate

An intiltration problem related to water quality occurs when the
normal ‘infiltration rate for the applied water or rainfall is appreci
Bly reduced and water remains on the soil surface too long or intil-
trates too slowly to Supply the Crop With sufficient water to maintain
acceptable yields. Although the infiltration rate of water into soil
varies widely and can bo greatly influenced by the quality of the
irrigation water, soil factors such as structure, degree of compaction,
organic mattor content and chemical maKO-up Can 180 greatly iREluence
“he intake rate.

The two most common water quality factors which influence the
pormal infiliration rate are the salinity of the water (total quantity
OF salts’ in the water) and its Sodium content relative to the calcium
ana ‘magnesiua content. A high salinity water will increase iniltra-
ion. A low salinity water or a water with a high sodium to calcium
atic will decrease infiltration. Sots factors may operate at the same
time. Secondary problems may also dovelop Lf LErigations must ba pro-
Longéa for an extended period of time to achieve adequate infiltration.

An infiltration provlen related to water quality in most cases

1.3 APPROACH TO CVALUATING WATKR QUALITY

The presiction that a vaser quality-related problem will occur

requires evaluation Of the SoLintisl of Lhe water to Sroete soil condi-

S6-

tions that may restrict its use or that may require the use of special

Of procedures available for this evaluation but Fogardicss of which one
is usea, ompnasia should focus on relating the potential problem to the
lola situation since solutions to water quality problems usually must
ba implemented at che farm level rather than at the project level. The
evaluation must therefore be done in farms of specific local conditions
5£"ute ana the farm management Capability of the water veer.

This approach is the same as in the 1976 dition of this paper
and similar guidelines are proposed for evaluating the potential of an
{frigation vater to create soil or crop problems. The guidelines are
£ollowed by suggestions on management alternatives to overcome those
potential problems. This approach is often referred to as a provlem-
Solving approach and emphasizes long-tern effects on irrigated agri-
Slture rather than short-term, because of the large investments now
heeced in irrigated agriculture.

The four problem categories previously discussed - salinity,
infiltration, toxicity and miscellaneous - are used for evaluation.
Water Guality problems, however, ars often complex and a Cosbination of
Drobloms may affect Crop production more sevoroly than a single problem
In’ {solation. The more complex the problem, the aore difficult it is to
formulate an economical management programme for solution.

If problems do occur in combination, they are more easily
understood and solved If each factor is considered individually.
Thorofore, the guidelines and discussion which follow treat each
problon and its Solution Soparatoly, so that a nusbor of factors are
Slaluated Tor each of the problem arsas, such ast

+ the type and concentration of salts causing the

provlen;

+ the soll-water-plant interactions that may cause

the Lows in crop yield
* the expected sevority of the problem following
Long-term use of the water:

+ the management options that are available to pre-

vent, correct, or olay the onset Of the problem.

2. SALINITY PROBLEMS
2.1 IvrRopuCTION

Irrigation water contains a mixture of naturally occurring
salts. Soils irrigated with this water will contain a similar mix but
Ssualiy at a higher concentration than in the applicd water. Tne extent
£0 which tno salts accumulate in the soil will depend upon the irriga-
tion water quality, ‘Lrrigation management and tho adequacy of drainage.
Tf salts become excessive, losees in yield will result. To prevent
yield loss, malts in the soil must be controlled at a concentration
Below that which might affect yield.

Most water used for irrigation is of good to excellent quality
and is unlikely to present serious salinity constraints: Salinity
control, however, becomes more Gifficult as water quality becomes
poorer. ‘As water ‘salinity increases, greater cars must be. taken to
Teach salts cut of tne root onc before their accumulation reaches a
concontration which might affect yields. Alternatively, steps must be
taken to plant crops tolerant to the expected Foot zone salinity. The
Erequency of leaching depends on water quality and the crop sensitivity
to salinity.

The intent of this chapter is to illustrate the effect of water
quality on the build-up of S511 salinity and show now the latter can
Foduce the soil-water availsvle to the crop. Tnis is followed by 4
Giscussion of how leaching: crop seloction and other management
techniques are used to make salinity control easier and allow greater
use of more saline water in irrigated agriculture. Emphasis will be on
how ‘to manage intermediate quality wator with slight to moderate
Fostrictions on use, as shown in Table 1. Such water could result in
nore severe problems if it is not properly managed. The sams management
Fochniques will apply to a poorer Quality water. but as quality worsens
the options for management become fewer
2.2. BUILD-UP OF SOIL SALINITY

Salts are added to the soll with each irrigation. These salts
il1 reduce crop yield if they accumulate in the rooting dopth to
Gamaging concentrations. The Crop removes much of th appliod water
Econ the soil to meet its ovapotranspiration demand (ET) but leaves
moss of the salt behind fo concentrate in the Shrinking volume of Soil.
Water. AL each irrigation, moro salt 1s addod with tho appliod water, A
portion of the added salt must be leached from the root zone befors the
Concontration affects Crop yield. Leaching ie done by applying suffi.
Clone water so that a portion percolates through and below the entire
Foot zone carrying with it a portion Of the accumulated salts. The
fraction of applicd ater that passes through tho entire rooting depth
ana porcolates below is called the leaching fraction (LF).

teaching . depth of water leached below the root zone (,)

Eraction (LF = 0.5) results in less salt accumulation than a lower
leaching fraction (LF = 0.1). IL the water salinity (ECa) and the
leaching fraction (LF) are Known or can be cstimated, both the salinity

2.3 SALINITY EFFECTS ON CROPS

The primary objective of irrigation is to provide a crop with
adequate and timely amounts of water, thus avoiding yield loss caused
by oxtondod periods of water stress during atagos of crop growth that
ace B0nsitive to water HOtages. Mowavr, Suring repeated iFrigations,
fhe salts in the irrigation water can accumulate in tho Soil, reducing
water available to th crop and nastoning the onset of a water shorte
ge.” Understanding how tnin occurs Will help suggest ways to counter
Fhe effect and reduce the probability of a loss in yield.

The plant extracts water from the soil by exerting an absorptive
force greater than that which holds tho wator to the soll. If the plant
cannot make sufficient internal adjustment and exert cnough force, it
27ho Wble 10 extract sufficient water and will suffer water stress.
his happens when the Sail becomes foo dry. Salt in the. soll-water
incressas the force the plant must exert fo extract vater and this
403i ional force is rotorred Lo an the osmotic eifect oF osmotic

potential. For example, if two otherwise identical soils are at the
Same water content but ono is salt-fres and th other is salty, the
plant can extract and use more water from tho salt-Iree soil than from
the salty ‘Soil. Tho reasons are not oasily explained. Salts have an
EEinity for water. Tf the water contains salt, More enersy per unit of
uater must be sxpended by the plant to absorb relatively sali-free
water from a relatively salty soll-vater solution.

For all practical purposes, the added enorgy required to absorb
water from the salty soll (osmotic potential) 1s additive co the energy
required to absorb water from a salt-reo soil (sofl-water potont isl).
The cumulative SEfect is illustrated in Figure 3 and results in an
important reduction in water available to the crop as salinity in
creases. Salinity oifects are closely analogous to those of drought as
both result in water stress and reduced growth. Stunting, leaf damage
and necrosis or obvious injury to the plant are only noticeable after
Prolonged exposure to relatively high salinity.

The previous discussion showed how the concentration of salts in
the soil varied with leaching fraction and depth in tho root zone and
resulted in an increase in concentration as the leaching fraction
Gocroasos oF with increasing dopth in the Foot some. As the Soil dries,
Ehe plant is also exposed Lo a continually changing water availability
in ach portion of the rooting depth since the soil-wator content
(SolT-vater potential) and scil-uater salinity (osmotic potential) aro
both changing as the plant uses water between iFFigationa. Tho plant
absorbs Wator but most Of tho salt is excluded and left behind in the
Foor zone in a shrinking volume Of soll-water. Figure 4 shows that
Eollowing an irrigation, the soil salinity Ln not constant with depth.
Following cach irrigation, the soil-water content at sach depeh in the
Foot z0n6 is near the maximum, and tho concentration of dissolved salts
is near the minimum. Bach changes, however, as water is used by the
crop between irrigations.

The plant exerts its absorptive force throughout the rooting
dopth and takes water from wherever most readily available (the least
resistance to absorption). Usually this is the upper root zone, the
area most frequently replonisnod by irrigation and rainfall. Since moro
Gator passes through thls upper root zone, Lt 1s more thoroughly
Toachod and the osmotic or salinity effects are much less than at
greater depths. Betueen irrigations, the Upper root zone dries more
Fapidly than the lower because of the proliferation of foots in this
20m which extract the readily available soil moisture. The plant must
Shon meet more of its water dmand from increasingly greater depths as
the’ uppor soil-water Ls doploted. Both tho soil moisture at dopth and
¢he S011 moisture remaining in the upper portions have & higher Soil-
water salinity and thus a greater ommotic potential. As the plant
depletes the Soil-water, a water extraction pattern develops: Tho
oxtraction pattern of 40, 90, 20 and 10 porcont for tho upper to lower
Quarters of the root tons 1s ‘assumed in the guidelines In Table 1. This
Slotoly fits water extraction patterns undor normal irrigation prac-
Tices and 1s assumed throughout this paper-

The pattorn for water uptako is closoly related to tho frequency
of irrigation. With infrequent irrigations, as assumed for tne guide:
Vines in Table 1, tho typical oxtraction pattern is 40-30-2010, but
for more frequent irrigations the water uptake pattern is skowed

2.4 MANAGEMENT OF SALINITY PROBLEVS

The objective of salinity corsrol a to maintain an acceptable
crop yield. Several maragement options are available for salinity
control and those will be discussed here as separate options, but in
practice a combination nay be hsed to Solve tne problem.

The preceding section discussec the importance of (1) leaching
salts out of tho root zone before they bulld up to levels that might
affect yields, and (2) wairtairing adequate soil-vacer availability at
all tines. Adequate drairage is equally inportant anc long-term
Salinity control 1s not otherwise possible. If drainage 1s adequate
fhe epth of water required for Liaching depends on the Salt Sensis
Eivity of the Crop and the salinity of the applied water. When salinity
is high, the depth Of leaching water needed tay be too grea, making it
necessary to change to a more salt tolerans crop. provided market
economics will allow this.’ In dealirg with a rajer Salinity problem
Felated to water quality, a cropping change ie corsicersc a drastic
Step and will only be taken when less sevare options have failed to
maintain economic production. Leaching, on the other hand, is a basic
Stop in production even for water of the beat quality and must be
practised when necassary to avoi¢ salt accurulation that could ulti
mately affect production. Leaching car only so. done, however, Lf the
Grainage below the crop root tone ie suficient to brevant a rise in
the water table to that it is not a source of salt by itself.

Drainage, leaching and changes to more salt tolerant crops are
used to avoid the impact of long-tera salinity build-up but other
Cultural practices may also be nooded zo deal with possible short-term
or temporary increases in salinity which pay be equally detrimental to
Crop yield. Many cultural practices such as more frequent irrigation,
Tand Grading, timing Of fert:liration ard n0thods Of 8008ing make
salinity management easier.

2.4.1 Drainage

Salinity probleas encountered in irrigated agriculture are
very frequently associated with an uncontrolled water table within
one to two metres of the ground surface (figure 5). In most soils
with a shallow water table, water rises into the active root sone
by capillarity and, Lf the vator table contains salts, it becomes a
Continual source of salts to the root zone ae water is used by the
Crop or evaporates at the soil surface. Salinisation from this
Source can be rapid in irrigated areas in hot climates where
portions of the land remain fallow for extondod periods. The rate
6 soll salinity accusilation from an uncontrolled ansilow water
fable Will dopend upon irrigation management, salt concentration
and Geptn of the groundwater, soil type, and climatic conditions.
Figure 6 shows that capillary rise trom a shallow water table can
represent a sizeable salt Lnpue into the root zone.

In arid and semi-aric climates, a salinity probles caused or
complicated by poor drainage cannot be ssequstely controlled until
tho wator table is stabilized and maintained at a mate depth -
usually at least tuo metres. This requires open or tile grains or
drainage wells to romovo a part of the salty subsuriace water and
transport it to an acceptable salt-sink for Saf disposal. When
drainage is adequate, salinity related directly to water quality
and ‘irrigation management becomes a problem only If the salts
applied with the irrigation water are allowed to accumulate to
Concentration which reduces yield. Effective salinity controls
therefore, must include adequate drainage to cantral and
stabilize the water table and leaching as needed to reduced tho
accumulated salis. A nec Gownvard flux Of surface applicd water to
achiove the Toquired leaching will then control the salinity. The
Guidelines in fable 1 and the remainder of the discussion in this
Paper assune that all salts accumulating in the crop root zono como
Trom the applicd water. This means drainage is adequate and
salinity management is a significant part of irrigation manage
2.4.2 salinity control by Leaching

When the build-up of soluble salts in the soil becomes or is
oxpoctod to bacomo Oxcossive, the salts can be leached by applying
mOFe water than that needed by the crop during the Growing season.
This oxtra vater moves at least a portion of the salts bolow the
root zone by deop percolation (1caching). Leaching is the key
factor in controlling Soluble salts brought in by the irrigation
water. Over tine, salt removal by leaching must equal Or exceed the
Salt additions from the applied water or salts will build up and
Gvontually reach damaging concentrations. The questions that arise
are hou much water should be used for leaching and whon should
Teachings bo applies?

Rainfall must be considered in estimating the leaching require
=ent. Rainfall that onters the soil is effective in meeting botn
Crop ET and the leaching requirement. Rainfall that infiltrates
into the soil (effective rainfall) replaces ET losses. If in
excess of ET, it becomes drainage water and will satisty part or
21170¢ "the leaching needed to control salts. The advantage of
Fainfall in accomplishing all or part of the leaching is thar it
uniformly applies an almost salt-fres water (ECw <0.05 45/m).
Loaching is further enhanced if the rate of rainfall (s belo
the Infiltration rate of the soil. If the total amount of rain.
£al1 infiltrated la suficiont, it will reduce the average
Salinity used for the applied water (£Cw) in calculating the
leaching requirement (LR) and thus reduce proportionately the
required leaching. Figure 8 shows how rainfall quickly reduces
ne salinity in the crop root zone.
In low rainfall years or low rainfall areas, precipitation may
fot be adequate Lo refill the soil to its water holding capa
ity,” in which case no leaching occurs to reduce accumulated
3alinity other than to move the salts from the upper part of the
Foot zone deoper into the soil. Tha upper portions af the root
ing dopth will then reflect the very low salinity levels of the
rainfall hich can enhance Germination.
In areas where rainfall occurs in the cooler months or winter
Season, it may bo possible to ennance winter leaching sven in a
Gry year. It is recommended that a heavy autumn or early winter
irrigation be given fo refill the soil profile with water before
the Faine. Winter rains will then complate the soil-wator ro-
plenishacnt ‘and accomplish all or part of the required leaching
ith low-salt water. If the rewscting or leaching is still not
Complete by crop planting Cime, the Gasp percolation losses from
Sxtonded early soason Lrrigations may accomplish the soil rower
ting and salt loaching. Figure 9 Shows how winter rains have
leached salts from citrus plantings in Cyprus.
The leaching requirement can be calculated (Bguation 3) but we
Can only make estimates of the amount Of leaching that le acty-
ally taking place. Soil and crop monitoring are useful tools to
Gtormine tho noed for leaching. Considerable variation occurs
From one cropping acason Lo the next; therefore, monitoring
Should stress long-ters trons and changes in soil salinity.
Several studies, field trials and observations suggest proce-
Guros that might increase the oificiency of leaching and reduce
She amount of water needed. Those will fot be covered in detail
here but will be mentioned as they apply to many irrigation
Situations:
. loach uring the cool scason instead of the warm to
increase the efficiency and ease of leaching since the ET
108658 aro lower:
. use more salt tolerant crops which require a lower LR and
“hus a lower total water demand:
© use tillage to slow overland water flow und reduce the
Rumbar of surface cracks which bypass flow throuah 1arge
pores and ecresse efficiency in leaching;
. use sprinkler irrigation at an application rate below the
051 Infiltration rato which favours unsaturates flow

iii. monitoring

A Good irrigation management plan strives to apply sufficient

Vater to mest the Crop water demand plus the leaching require-

ont without wastage. Both tha crop wator demand and leaching

Foquirement can bo estimated and the depth of applied water

needed can be calculated. In many instances, however, estimates

Of deptn of applied water (flow rate, duration and area covered)

are inaccurate or not availasle, making estimates of effective

Ross of leaching for salinity control unreliable. Existing

Conditions and reliable estimates of past management can be

dotormined with a reasonable degree of cortainty by means of

Soil Samples, analysed for salinity. From che soil samples an

apparent lehching fraction as well as an average root zone

salinity resulting from past irrigation practices can be

Geverninea.

The following procedure is suggested:

a. Estimate the probable depth of rooting of the last crop
grown - from observation (pit, hole, soil samples, otc.)
Or from past experience. Depth estimate should include 75
To 83 percent of the observed root zone.

5. Take representative soil samples from cach quarter depth of
Foot zone and analyse sach Guarter depth Separately for ECe
BY the soil pasts extraction metnod of the US Salinity
Laboratory (USDA 1954).

©. Plot by a graph sinilar to that of Figure 2 for the aoil
depth and salinity representative of cach quarter dopth of
Foot 2one and combars. this curve with tho curves dopicting
the various typical leaching fractions (LF = 0.1, 0.15,
0.2,0.3, 0.4) in the grapn. Then, ostimate a leaching
Fraction ‘for the site sampled based on the shape of the
salinity profile.

4. From the Ce of the four soil samples (one from cach
quarter depth of the root zone) calculate the average Foot
Zomo salifity and compare with tho crop tolerance ECs
Values in Table 4 for the crops to bo planted.

©. From the apparent leaching fraction and the average £Co of
the oot zone, make any ecossary management decisions to
G3uce ireigations to increase or decrease the leacming
fraction in order to stay Close to the tolerance of the
preferred crop. Alternatively, change the crop to agree
nore closely with the oxisting salinity conditions.

2.4.3 Crop Tolerance to salinity

All plants do not respond to salinity in a similar manner; some
crops can produce accoptable yiclds at much greater soil salinity than
thers. This is because some aro better able to make the needed Osmotic

ability of the Crop to adjust to salinity is extremely useful. In areas
whore a build-up of soil salinity cannot be controlled at an acceptable
Concentration for the crop being grown. an alternative crop can be
Selected that is both more tolerant of the expected soil salinity and
Can’ produce economical yields,

There is an 8 to 10-£0ld range in salt tolerance of agricultural
crops. This wide range in tolerance allows for a much greater use of
moderately saline water much of which was previously thought to be
unusable. It also greatly expands the acceptable range of water
Salinity (C4) considered suitable for irrigation.

The relative salt tolerance of most agricultural crops is known
well enough to give general salt tolerance guidelines. Tolerances for
many common field, vegetable, forage and tree crops are given in Table
3. nis table has boon updated from the 1976 edition and gives the
Latest tolerance values for crops grown under semi-arid irrigated
agriculture. Where insufficient data exist to give numerical values for
tolerance, a relative rating has beon assigned to the crop, based on
field oxporionce, limited data or observations. For comparative
purposes, relative tolerance ratings are listed in fable 5 for a large
Rumber of craps, including many Of those given in Table 4. General
Groupings for tolerance are Shown in the schematic disgras in Figure
10." ne" relative tolerance ratings, even if based on a limited amount
of data, ‘are useful for comparisons among cropa.

The relative crop tolerance ratings were considered in setting
the degrees of ‘restriction on use’ in the Guidelines of Table 1. for
example, the tolarance data of Table 4 indicate that a full yield
potential should be obtainable for nearly all crops when using a water
Which has a Salinity less than 0.7 ds/m. The guidelines of Table 1
Indicate that water of this salinity would have no restriction on use.
For tho salinity listed in the slight to moderate range, a full yield
potential is still possiblo but Care must bo taken to achieve the
Fequired leaching fraction in order to maintain soil salinity within
the tolerance of the crop. For higher salinity water and sensitive
crops, increasing the leaching to satisfy a leaching requirement
groastor ‘than 0.25-0.30 may not be practical bocause of tho oxcossive
Gmounc of water required. In such a case, consideration must bo given
£0 changing to a more tolerant crop that will require less leaching to
control salts within crop tolerance. As the water salinity (ECw)
increases within the Slight to moderate range, production of the more
sensitive Crops may be restricted due to an inability to achiove tho
high leaching fraction needed, especially when grown on tne heavier,
Boro clayoy Soil types. IE the salinity of the applied water excesds
3%" as/m, as Shown in Table 1 for a Aevere restriction on use, the
water may still bo usable but its uso may need to be restricted to more
permeanit Soils and more salt folerant crops where the high leaching
Fractions are more sasily achieved.

The salt tolerance data of Table 4 are used in the calculation
of the leaching requirement. Figuro 7 can also be used to sstinate the
Teaching requirement if crop tolsrance grouping and water salinity sre
known, as 4i3cusscd in the previous section. If the exact cropping
patterns or rotations are ot knoun for a now area, the leaching
Foquirement must be based on the loast toiorant of the crops adapted to
Eh ara. In those inétances whore soil salinity cannot he maintained
within accoptable limits of proforred sensitive crops, changing to more
Tolorant crops ulll Fass tho arca's production potential. In case of
Goubt a5 fo the offect of the water salinity on crop production, a
Pilot study should bo undertaken to domonsirate the feasibility tor
Terigation and the outlook for economic Success.

4. Factors affecting tolerance

Crop production potential using a particular irrigation water
can range from 100 percent down to sero but there are often
Factors other than water quality wich affect yielo. The
tolerance values in Table 4 represent production potential when
salinity 1s the only limiting factor. Such conditions, however,
G0 not Always exist. Other conditions may also limit production
but the relative yield lose duc to salinity will approsimace
those in Table 4 if salinity is the main limiting factor.
The soil salinity tolerances in Table 4 apply primarily to crops
from late seedling Stage to maturity. Tolerance during the Gor-
mination and early seedling stage may be different and is only
Clearly defined for a fow crops. Tabls 7 presents data for a few
Crops showing soil salinity thai resulted in a 50 percent reduc.
Sion in either yield or seedling smergence. In ganeral, if tne
soil salinity in tho surface soil (vooding area) iv groator than
27as/m, it may innibit or delay germination ang early seedling
Growth. this slowed germination may thon delay tacegence, allow
Tng"S0i1 crusting and alscasc probicas to reduce the Crop stand.
Rainfall or pre-plant irrigations will often help to maintain
Low salinity, delay crusting and prosote good emergence.

2.4.4 cultural practices
The primary management options co control salinity were dis

cussed in the preceding sections: adequate arainage, leaching to

--- PÁGINA 56 ---
Sa
control salinity within the tolerance of the crop or, Lf this camot be
Gone, change to a more salt tolorant crop that requires loss leaching
for adequate salt control. These management practices are the ones most
appropriate for long-term salinity control but there are soparate
Ciftural practices that can have a profound effect upon germination,
arly seedling growth and ultimately on yiold of crop. Low yields are
Gfton the result of obtaining poor crop stands during the germination
Or" Barly ssdling stage of growen. These short-term cultural practices
hat aid in salinity control become more iportant as th irrigation
water salinity increases, and are often done on an annual or continual
Danis. They include land smoothing for better wator distribution,
Vining of Irrigations to prevent crusting and water stress, placement
ot mead to avoid areas likely to be 8alin zed, and care in selection of

i. Land smoothing or graging

Substitute for land levelling to a sot gradient or slope. Land

To ensure uniform water distribution when annual crops arc
Changed, In contrast, land grading is usually a one-time

iv. rertilization
Fortilizors, manures, and soil smendmonts include many soluble
S31ts in nigh concentrations. If placed coo closs to tne
germinating seedling or to the growing plant, the fertilizer may
alse or ‘aggravate a aalinity or toxicity problem. For example:
an application of 50 kg por hectare of nitrogen (240 kg/ha of
Zmnon lum aulphate) “would cause no salinity problem if spread
Gniformly over a one hectars area. Howover, if drilled with the
seed at planting tine, it would probably reduce germination or
growth of seedlings and might result in crop failure caused by
he high salinity of the fertilizer placed too close to tne
seed.
Care, therefore, should be taken in placement as well as timing
of Fertilization. Seedlings are sensitive to salts and, while
Small, require little fertilization. A small amount Of farti-
Tizec ‘can be applicd at of before planting, and tho remainder in
one or more spplications after crop emergence but before the
Rain growth period. Tn addition, a fertilizer with a lower salt
Thdex’ can bo considered. The lower the salt index of tho
£orcilizor, the less danger there is of salt burn and damage to
seedlings or young plants. Salt Indices for various fertilizers
2£e shown in Table 9.
Salt tolerance of a crop is generally considered to be un-
Sffected by raising tho lovel of soil fertility above that
Rocessary to Supply needed nutrients for optimum growth.
However, if both Salinity and Low fertility are limiting yield,
Correction of either cr both will improve yield. 1, however,
The Fertility is adequate and the salinity is limiting, further
increasing tho Fortilicy will not Incroass yield or improve the
salt tolerance of the crop (Bornstein, Francois and Clark
1974).

Isolated pockets of accumulated salt frequently result where
water does not infiltrate sufficiently to accomplish leaching. These
Can be raised areas, aroas Of more dense soil, or areas for getting
enough water during ‘irrigation. Typically these show as bare spots or
areas of reduced or stunted growths A well designed sprinkler system
Generally provides the greatest uniformity of application, but this is
Seton a problom no matter which system is used.

Each irrigation method has certain advantages and disadvantages
and all known factors should be considered before attempting to. improve
Salinity control by changing tho method,

With surface flooding methods (flood, basin and furrow), depth
of applied water ontering the SOLL varies with location in the {161d
na "Gepends on the infiltration rate and time available for intilo
tration. Differonces in the rate of infiltration are caused by land
slope, degre of compaction, textural changes, and soil chemistry.
The. opportunity time during which infiltration can take place ales
varies; the upper end of the field nearest the water Source usually
has water on its surface for a much longer time than doos the lover
end.” The driest area is typically abot two-thirds of the Gistance
Gown the field. High spots in tne field also receive less water
because, being high, they are covered by loss wator and for a shorter
period.

Those surface flooding methods are usually not sufficiently
€loxible £0 apply less than an 80 co 100 nm depth per irrigation. As 4
result, irrigating more frequently to reduce possible water stress may
3150 waste water ana cause waterlogging and drainage problems. In order
To relieve water stress, it may be taser to increase the frequency of
irrigation with sprinklers or drip irrigation rather than with surface
Elooding. Nouever: sprinkler and localized irrigation have their
problems too and are not adapted to ail conditions of waters soil,
Climate, or type of crop.

A well-designed sprinkler systos will apply water with good
uniformity and at rates of application low Snough to prevent runoff If
wel) managed, ic will result in an excellent overall irrigation and
Rdequate and unifora leaching. Depth of sater applied is controlled by
a0justments in the duration of application, sprinkler spacing and
nozzle size. Wing can stort tho water patterns and must bo con-
Siderea. Sprinklers are ‘scastimes used to aid in temporature concrol,
gormination and early seedling growth at Which Lime tho crop may bo
Barticularly ‘Sensitive to salinity, nigh tomparaturss snd soil Crust
ing. on sensitive crops, however, sprinklers can cause lcat burn Lf the
ailis (sodium or cnloride) concentrate excessively on the leaves as
ater ovaporatos between rotations of tne sprinkler. Those salts arc
absorbed ana may cause a Lowicity. These problems arc discussed in
Gotail in Section 4.3.

One of the concerns expresses about sprinkler use in hot arid
aroas Ls the evaporation Loss during sprimkling and the possible
Snerease in salinity of the water that infiliraces the soil, but there
is no clear evidence that this evaporation is significant enough to
“warrant concern. Ono £icld study in the Imperial Valloy of California,
USA, using a solid set sprinkler system, showed that evaporation losses
on a hot, dry cay (temperature 47'C, relative humidity 27 percent and

(xCw) “Near the fisla sage but loss than 5 percent within tne sain

4. Inproved subsurface drainage
Subsurface drainage problons and shallow water tables greatly
complicate salinity control. Shallow water tables frequently
ocCur duo to the prosence of a slowly permosblo layer below tho
Soil surface such'as a clay barrier, hardpan or bedrock. brain
ago problems are moat froquently caused by over irrigation but
may 2150 be Caused by secpage from upslope areas or leakage from
Canals. The most effective control of salinity associated with a
hallow water table is Eirst to lover and stabilize the water
fable. A discussion of drainage needs is presented in Section
2.4.1" as one Of the primary options considered for controlling
salinity caused by poor quality water. An in-depth discussion of
drainage noods, Surveys and designs is given: in Dieleman and
Tratfors (1976) and Dicleman ot al. (1980) If new land 1a being
brought into production, drainage must be considered, ana it i
ossential for ‘the long. term success of any irrigation project or
irrigated ara. If drainage problems are in any bay to be
anticipatea, plans for their immediate or Future control must be
formulated.’ With adequate drainage established, surface soil
salinity can be controlled by LErigation management.

3. INFILTRATION PROBLEMS

3.01 THE INFILTRATION PROSLEM

An inciltration problem occurs If the irrigation water does not
enter the soil rapidly enough durirs a normal irrigation cyole to
Feplenish the soil with water nosded by £18 crop bafors the next
irrigation. The Teducad Infiltration rate, Lf due to qualicy of applied
water, is generally a problon wizain the upper fow centimetres of soil
but occasionally may occur at greater Gepcha. The end result is a
decrease in water supply te the Crop, siailar to the reduction due to
salinity, but for a Gifforent reason. A water infiltration provles
reduces’ the quantity of watef pus Ifto the soil for later use by tne
Crop while salinity reduces the availability of the water in storage.

Infiltration refers to he ercry of water into the soil. The
Fate at which water enters in reforred to an the rate of infileration.
Permeability, the tern used in the previous edition of Irrigation and
Drainage Paper 29 (19761, moro correctly refers to the percolation of
infiltrated water throug tno soil. Since the water quality problem is
primarily one related to the ase with #aich water enters and moves
Through the upper few contiretres of Boil, we nave chosen the ters
“infiltration probler rather than the provicusly used ter 'permesbi
Lity problem’. An inflliratios rate as low as 3 ma/hour is considered
lou while a rate above 12 ma/hosr 1s relatively high. This can be
affected, nowevor, by many factors osaor than water Guality, including
physical characteristics of the soil, suca as Soil texture and type of
Clay “minerals, and chemical characteristics including exchangeable
Cations. The guidelines of Table 1 refer to infiltration problems as
they relate directly to the unfavourable charges in soil chemistry
caused by the quality of ireigation water applied. These provisas
concern both salinity and relacive sodium concent in the applies water.
Figure 21 shows in graphic form thas sotn salinity (EC) and the sodium
adSorption ratio (SAR) Of the appiiod Water affect the rae of infil.
Eration of water into surtace sail. Figure 21 can be used in place of
the numerical evaluations in Tasle 1 given for infiltration problems.

The infiltration rate gencrally increases with increasing
salinity and docreases with sithor Gecreasitg salinity or increasing
sodium Content relative to calcium and magnesiue = the sodium a0sorp
Elon ratio. Therefore, the two factors, salinity and SAR, must be
considered together for a proser evaluation of the ultimate effect on
ater infiltration rate.
3.0.1 Infiltration problen fvaluation

d5/m) is corrosive and tends to loach surface soils free of soluble
ninorals and salts, oapecially calolum, reducing thoir strong stabi-
Tixing influence on sail agaregates ard soil structure. Without salts
and without calcium, the soi) Gisporses and the dispersed finer soil
part fclos £111 many of tno analler pore spaces, sealing the surface and
Greatly reducing the rate at whic water infiltrates the soil surface.
Soil crusting and crop srergence proslers ofien result, in addition to
reduction in the amourt of vater the: will enter the soil in a given
Amount Of time and which nay Ultimscely cause water stress betwaen
Irrigations.

invariably results in water infiliration problems, regardless of the
relative sodium ratio (or SAR). Rainfall 1s a very low salinity water

3.2 MANAGEMENT OF INFILTRATION PROBLEMS

Irrigating to £111 the soil-water reservoir for later withdrawal
by the crop is GifEicult when infiltration rates are low, but there is
Ueele eed to “take action to incroase infiltration ules tho crop
water demand or ‘the leaching requirerent cannot be met. Water ponding
for too long a time frequently olves rise to Secondary problems nich
are ac important in causing yield loss as is an actus water snortage
an often dotersine renedial measures co correct the infiltration
proslea. Some of the more Sroublosore secondary provlems are crusting
56 seed bad, excousive weed grows and suriace saturation which can
Cause root rot, diseases, Rucritional disordees, scor Aeration and poor
germination. In somo cases, water ponding for an excessive period of

3.2.3 cultivation and Deep Tillage

S011 and water amendments and blending change the chemical
nature Of the water while physical methods keep the soil pan by
mechanical moana. The most common physical method in either cultivation
OF Goep tillage. Both are sffectiva but normally short-lived and are
Shs only temporary solutions to 4 water in€iltration problon.

Cultivation is usually dome for weed control or soil aeration
rather than to improve uater penctration. Whore infiltration problems
are severe, cultivation or tillage are helpful ss thoy rougnon tne
SUrface and slow the low of water, increasing the time during which
iniltration can take place. A rough, cloady furrow or fiela improves
ineiltration Guring the first one or two (rrigations, After hich
another cultivation may be nacced. Cultivation equipment can often bo
modified to leave a rougher surface. Cultivation breaks up the crust in
the upper fou Gentimetres Of soil to improve infiltration. A common
practice in areas where o water infiltration problem hat been caused by
Fou salinity ‘water is to cultivate before Gach irrigation of sefors
overy second irrigation. This rougnens the soil and opens cracks and
air Spaces that greatly increase the Surface arca cxposed for infiltra-
tion.

beeper tillage (chiselling, subsoiling) can be oxpected to
improve deep wator penetration for only one oF two irrigations since
he SOL surface soon reverts to Its ofiginal condition but, although
improvement is not permanent, this practice may temporarily allow
sufficient water to Cater to make an appreciable difference in stored

and rips tho soll, and is dot prior to planting or during periods of
Gormancy whon roof pruning or root disturbances of permanent crops ars

3.2.4 Organic Residues

Crop residues or other organic matter left in the field will
improve water penetration and is becoming a more widoly accepted
practice. It is ono of the easiest methods to improve water infiltra-
Tion, especially’ for small farmers who do not Rave the resources to
implement more costly corrective measures. Unfortunately, in many
instances, ‘the small farmers se Crop residues for other purposes and
little, if any, is returned to the soil.

Crop residues left on the soil or worked into a rough cloddy
8011 surface will improve water penetration on sodic soils and will
also improve water panctration into soils being irrigated by high SAR
or low salinity water. Both crop residue loft on the soil surface as
well 2s the root ‘system of the crop help in keeping the soil open. The
benefits decline with time until replenished at the noxt cropping

The more lbrous ana less easily decomposed crop residucs, as
from barley, rice, wheat, maize and sorghum, nave improves water
penetration, whereas residues from legumes and vegetable crops gener
211 have not. The best residues are those which Go not decompose or
broak down rapidly. These Koop the soi: porous by maintaining open
Channels and voiod which improve water penetration: To be SELectivas
Felatively large quantities of rosiducs are noodeds for instance.
manure has been used at rates of 40 to 400 metric tons per hectare to
improve water infiltration. An organic application in the range of 10-
30" percent by 3011 volume in the upper 15 cm of soil may be needed to
be otfective.

Where water quality is affecting water infiltration, anc
organics are boing tried to improve infiltration, it is important to
(ndorporats the residues into the surface few centimetres of Soil.
Dooper incorporation is beneficial for moil structure and desper
percolation of applied water, but for infiltration problems Causca by
water quality, it is the surface soil that usually controls the depth
OF Water entering the soil in a given period of time.

Rice hulls, sawdust, shredded back, and many othor waste
products have been used in largo volumes but with varying dogrecs of
Success. Tents with rice hulls in India increased the yield of rice in
the first cropping season but yields reverted to thelr original level
when the troatmont was discontinued. Pros a long-torm standpoint, the
Eeturn Gf organic matter to the Soil helrs maintain soil structure and
returns necdod nutrionts, but using a high rate of organic matter also
causes problems. These incluce nutritional upsets, salinity effects
Caused By salty manure, nitrogen shortages or excesses owing to the use
Gf Cortain types Of matorials (manures vo sawdust) and toxicities
(chloride and poassium toxicities from rico hulls).

4.1 SPECIFIC TONS AND THEIR EFFECTS

A toxicity problem is different from a salinity problem in that
it occurs within tho plant (self and is not caused by a water shore-
age. Toxicity normally results when Cortain ons are taken up with the
S0ii-water and accumulate in the leaves during water transpiration to
an extent that results in damage to the plant. The degres of damage
depends upon time, concentration, crop sensitivity and crop water uses
and if Gamage is Severs enough, crop yield is reduced. The ubual toxic
fons in irrigation water are Shloride, sodium and boron. Damage can be
caused by each, individually of in combination.

Not all crops are oquilly sensitive to these toxic fons. Most
annual crops are not sensitive at the concentrations shown in Table 1
but the majority Of tree crops and woody perennial-type plants are.
Toxicity synptoms, however, can appear on almost any crop Lf concentra
tions are high snough. Toxicity often accompanics of complicates a
salinity or infiltration problem although it may appear oven when
Salinity is lou.

The toxic ions sodium and chloride can also be absorbed dirsctly
into the plant through the leaves moistened during sprinkler irriga
tion. This occurs typically during periods of high temperature and low
humidity. Tho loaf absorption speeds the rate Of accumslation Of a
Hoxic Lon and may be 8 primary source of the fomicity.

Many trace elements, in addition to sodiun, chloride and boron,
are toxic to plants at very low concentrations. Fortunately most
Irrigation supplies contain very low concontrations of these trace
clemdnts and are generally not a probles. Suggested maximum concentra.
Tions for these unususl trace elements are given in Gection 5.5. Those
Concentrations are bated upor limits ssraniished to protect the soil
Fosource’ from contamination if continuously irrigated with water which
Contains them.

40.0 chloride

The most common toxicity is from chloride in the irrigation
water. Chloride is not adsorbed of held hack by soils, therelore i¢
moves readily with tho soil-water, is taken up by the crop, moves in
the transpiration stream, and accumulates In tho loaves. If th
chloride concentration in the leaves exceeds the tolerance of the crop,
injury symptoms Govolop such ai leaf burn or drying of loaf tissue.
Normally, Slant injury occurs first at th leaf tis (unich is comon
for chloride toxicity), and progresecs from the tip back along the
edges as scverity increases. Excessive necrosis (dead tissue) is often
accompanied by sarly leaf drop or defoliation. With sensitive crops,
Chose symptoms occur when leaves accumulate trom 0.3 fo 1.0 porcont
Chloris on 3 dry welgnt bails, but sensitivity varies among those
Crops. Many tro ‘ropa; for oxamplo, Login to show Lnjury above 0.3
percent chloride (dry weight).

5 ncarby undamaged ones for comparative purposes.

4.0.3 soron

Boron, unlike sodium, is an essential element for plant growth.
(Chloride is’ also essential but in such small Quantities that it is
frequently classed non-essential.) Boron 1s needod in relatively small
amounts, however, and if present in amounts appreciably greater than
necded, it becomds toxic. For some crops, if 0.2 mg/l boron in water is
essential, T to 2 mg/l may 50 toxic. Surface water Farely contains
enough boron to be toxic but wall water or springs occasionally contain
Eoxic amounts, especially near geothermal areas and earthquake faults.
Boron problems Originating fron the water are probanly more frequent
than those originating in tho Soil. Boron toxicity can atfect nearly
all crops but, like Salinity, there is & wide range of tolerance smony
crops.

Boron toxicity symptoms normally show first on older leaves as a
yollowing, spotting, of drying of Leaf tissue at the ips and edges.
Drying and chlorosis often progress toward the cantrs betueen the veins
(intorveinal) as mors and more boron accumulates with timo. On serious
iy affected trees, such aa almonds and other trae crops which da not
Show typical leaf symptoms, a gum or exudate on limbs or trunk is often
Roticoablo.

Most crop toxicity symptoms occur after boron concentrations in
Leaf blades exceed 250-300 my/kg (ry weight) bue not all sensitive
crops accumulate boron in eat blades. For example, stone fruits
(peaches, plums, almonds, otc.), and pone fruits (apples, pears and
others) are easily damaged by boron but they Go not accumslate Fufi-
iene baron in the leal tissue for leaf analysis to be a reliable
diagnostic test. With those crops, boron excess must be confirmed from
Soil and water analyses, tree symptoms and Growth characteristics.

A wide range of crops was tested for boron tolerance by using
sanc-culture techniques (Eaton 1944): Previous boron tolerance tables
Tn general use have been bancd for the mout part on these data. These
cables reflected boron tolerance at Which foxicity symptoms wers ire:
observed and, depending on srop, covered one to three soasons of
Dreigation: The ofiginal data from these early experiments, pls data
fron many othor sources, have recently boon revicwed (Wass 1984). Table
16 presents this recent’ revision of tho data. Tt Ls not based on plant
symptoms, but upon a significant loss in yield to be expected if the
halcated boron valuo is oxceddod. Table ‘17 presents recent data on
citrus and stone fruit rootstosks and are listed in order of increasing

4.2.2 crop Selection

Selecting a more tolerant crop offers a very practical solution
to a toxicity problem. Thors are degrees of sensitivity to boron.
chloride ana sodium just as there are degrees of sensitivity to
Salinity. Limited inforsation is available on the relative tolerance of
crops to toxic fons. Table 14 presents dita for chloride, Table 15 for
sodium, “and Tables 16 and 17 for boron. It must be kept in mind that

Factors affecting tolerance include climate, irrigation management,
1eaching Traction, drainage, Groweh stage of the crop and crop maturity

5.0 excess NITROGEN

Nitrogen is a plant nutrient and stimulates crop growth. Natural
8011 nitrogen or added fortilizora are the usual sources, but nitrogen
in the irrigation water has much the Same effect as soil-applied
gortilizer nitrogen and an excess will cause problems, Just as too much
fertilizer would. If excessive quantities are present or applied,
production of several Commonly grown crops may bo. upaet because of
Sver-stinulation of growtn, delayed maturity of poor quality.

The most readily available forms of nitrogen are nitrate and
ammonium but nitrate (NO, -N) occurs most frequently in itfigation
Vater. Awmonium-nitrogen Ls seldom present in excess of 1 mg/l unless
ammonia fertilizer or wastewater is being added to the water supply.
The concentration in most surface and groundwater 1s usually less than
5 59/1 NOy-N but some unusual groundwater may contain quantities in
excess of ‘$0 mg/l. Drainage water from bolow the root fone froquently

tion  programse. Wastowster, especially from £008 processing and

Compar sons Can be made. For sxasple, Noy. means nitrogen in the form

Coported as 10 mg/l No,-N or 10 mg/L H.-A). In the guidelines of Table
1,70 is roportod as nitrogen in the form of nitrate (NO-N) because
this is the Usual form Found in natural water.

Sensitive crops may be affected by nitrogen concentrations shove
5 m9/1. Mast other crops are relatively unaffected until nitrogen
exceeds 30 ma/l- For exanple. sugarbeots, a sensitive crop, increase in
Size with cxcessive nitrogen fertilization but the sugar content and

The sensitivity of crops varies with the growth stage. High

Cause yield losses Guring the later Flowering and fruiting scages. High

Less than 5 mg/l N has little effect, even on nitrogen sensitive
crops, but may stimulate nuisance growth of algas and aquatic plants in

Can occur when temperature, Sunlight and other nutfionts are opt imum,

increasés maintenance costs for clearing vegetation from canals and
drainage channels.

Denitrification to remove NO)-N trom the water supply bofore use
may be the only other alternative but is not used because of the high
Cost ‘of equipment and energy. Since nitrogen ia a valuable resource it
Should be utilized Lf possible.

5.4 MAGNESIUM pROBLEWS

Soils containing high levels of exchangeable magnesium are often
thought to be. troubled with soil infiltration probime. The role of
magnes ius in causing or partly causing these problems is not well
Gocumented but researchers from several Lrfigated areas have studied
tho problem. At present thers is reasonably good agreement that mag
nesin acts on soils in a uay which is More Like caloium than sodium,
and that it is preferentially adsorbed by the soil to a much greater
Gegree than sodilm but to a slightly less degree than calcium.

In a magnesium dominated water (ratio of Ca/Mg <1) or a magne-
sium soil ‘(soil-water ratio of Ca/Mg <1), the potential offect of
Sodium may Bo slightly increased. In other words, a given SAR value
will Show s1igntly more damage if the Ca/Mg ratio is less than 1. The
Lower the ratio, tho more damaging {s the SAR. Research findings show
ERIC at a given SAR of tho applied water, 3 higher soil ESP than normal
will Tosult whon using a water with a Ca/Mg ratio less than 1 (Ranman
and Bowell 1975)

One concern, however, is that productivity is sometimes reported
to be Low on high magnesium soils or on oils boing irrigated with high
magnesium vater even though (nlltration problems may fot be svident.
The effect may be due to 4 magnosium-induced calcium deficiency caused
by high levels of oxchangeabio magnesius in the soil. Some research
evidence shows that yiolds of crops such as barley, wheat. maize and
Bugarbeots are roducod when the Ca/Mg ratio in soil-vater is less than
one. The function of calcium in plants is not totally understood, but
calcium appears to reduce possible toxicities due to othor ions (Na,
13) in the Foot environment. If the Ca/Wy ratio is near or less than 1;
the uptake and translocation of Ca from soil-ator to the above-ground
parts of the growing crop is diminished due to antagonistic offects of
Righ magnesium or competition for absorption sites to such an extent
that Toss calcium in absorbed. A calcium deficiency may then be
experienced at a higher calciun concentration in the applied water or
in woll-water than would occur if the Ca/Ma ratio woro Bighor.
Although not definitely confirmed, it can 5 anticipated that irric
gation water with a similar ratio (Ca/hy <1) will produce 4 similar
Gffoct if a readily available source of Calcium is not present in the
oils

Other limited research indicates that the ratio of calcium to
total cations in the soilwator may also be critical. A calcium to

hooded for Optimum Toot Growth of barley ‘and cotton.

5.5.3 Evaluation criteria

With the high retention rate in the soil ana the low use by
plants, ideally tho maximum application rate should not excead that
Shich will allow normal crop Growth and Still not exceed any allowable
Concentration in the produce Coming from the fiold. Suggested maximum

27 Thos concontrations ars set because of concern for long-term
build-up of trace elements in the oil and for protection of the

iFrigation practices, these suggested levels should provent a build-up
hac might Timit futuro crop production or ut ilizatibn of tne products
Whether vastowator is used as all or only a part of the supply will not
=0Aify these Guidelines as they are based on protection of the soil
Fasource to assure its present and futurs production capability.

The guidelines reflect the current information available but as
they are supported by only limited, long-term Eleld experience, they
are’ necessarily conservative, which means that, if the Suggested limit
13° oxcoodod, a phytotoxicity still may not Secor. The suggested limits
in Table 21’ are’ to ensure that the site can he Used for all potential
crops in the futuro. It is recommended that the values be considered as
the maximus long-term average concentration based upon normal irriga-
tion appiication rates. Whon moro reliable dats become available, the
Tovels may be adjusted. If water above or close to the levels given in
Table 21 is considered for ise, an up-to-date review of mors recent
information Ln suggested to prevent possible future problems.

5.6 NUTRITION AND WATER QUALITY

Water quality has been discussed in this paper based upon four
different effects on crops of soils: salinity, reduced water infiltra-
tion, toxicity, or effects related to a Group of miscellancous water
Constituents. hose effects tomotimes cause nutritional imbalances or
IRtoractions which result in nutritional Lebalances.

5.6.1 Nutrition snd Salinity

Excessive salinity stunts the crop by reducing the availasility
of soil-wator, slowing crop growth and restricting root doveloment.
With higher salinity water, sodius and enloride toxicity are alse
Likely to be evident. As long as the crop is well supplicd witn
Eortilizor oloments, application Of xtra nutrionts to combat the
salinity effects will not improve yield. However, if nutrients such as
Aitrogen are in short supply, raising the nutrient level will usually
improve yield. Saline arces in the field are normally dark green to
blue-green, indicating that thoy are well supplicd with nitrogen. If
yellow, additional nitrogen should improve yield.

Most fertilizers, however, are water soluble salts and placement

Another cause of clogging is chemical precipitation of materials
such as lime (Cac0)) and phosphates (Ca,(?0,):). Normally this is
Gradual and difficult to locate. High temperatures or high ph are
Geuslly part Of the precipitation problem. Precipitation can result
Fron an ‘excess of calcium Or magnesium carbonates and sulphates, or
fron iron which ia in the ferrous form but hen in contact with oxygen
§370xiaiz0d to the insoluble ferric form (reddish-brown precipitate):

The tendency of a water to cause calcium precipitation can bo
predicted although there Ls no proven prectical method to svaluate how
Eerious the problem will be since it Goponds upon many factors. A First
approximation of the calcium precipitation can be made using the
Saturation index of Langelior which simply says that upon reaching the
Caloiun saturation point in the presence of Bicarbonats, lime (CacO:)
il] precipitate from the solution. Tho caturation index is defined as
The actual pi Gf the water (pha) minus he theoretical ph (pic) that
the water could have If in equilibrium wiih Caos.

Saturation Index = pHa - pHc as)

Positive values of the index (pHa > pHe) indicate a tendency for
Caco, to precipitate from the water whereas negative values indicate
thatthe vator will dissolve Cac. The value of pHa is obtained from
Taboratory Gata, while phe is estimated using the procedures described
15 Table 25. Al) water ‘having positive valuos should bo considered as
potential problem water [or use through drip systems and the need for
Preventative measures should be conaidorsd in Gesign. For oxample, an
Treigation water with a measured pi Of 7.7, Ca = 3.65 me/l, HCO = 3.80
me/1 and total salts of 8.23 me/l (Ca + Wg + Na) Vill have a theoroti-
ai pn of 704, Giving a saturation index of +0.3, which indicates a
Possibility of carbonate (line) precipitation. This may or may not
Feault ina plugging problem but LE tho pi is adjusted to 7.0 by acid
addition, the saturation index becomes -0.4 and carbonate precipitation
Should not Gccue. From Table 24, a problem is much moro likely at
measured ph greater than pi = 8.0; this is the DH of water close to
“equilibrium with finely ground Limestone (Cac0y).

Iron is moro difficult to evaluate for ite clogging potential as
it is frequently a contributor to other provlems, especially those of
iron bacterial slime. The limitation given In Table 21 of 5 3/1 should
be considered a maximum for Grip irrigation systems but, in practical
Varma, a value above 2.0 may be near maximum since filtration costs
becont excessive above this Iimit. h corcenteation of 0.5 3/1 should
be considered a potential problem if tannin-like compounds (often in
acid waters) or total Aulphides exceed 2 mg/l. The combination of the
fio normally produces undesirable slime growtns.

To prevent iron precipitation in lines or at the emitters iron
should be precipitated and filtered out beforo it enters the irrigation
System. In order to LLItor out the iron, it muse First be oxidized to
She ineolunie form, usually by chlorination, to a residual of 1 mg/l
Chlorine. An alfofnativo method i aeration in an opon pond or by
Thject ion of air into the water supply by mechanical means. This causes
oxidized iron to precipitate. Thon it can bo filtered and removed
Safors the water enters the irrigation line. Both are expensive and
TEficult processes and the practicalit; of treatment plus filtering
Should bo evaluated.

Many cases of clogging have occurred from biological growths
inside the irrigation lines and openings. Theme are caused by small
quantities of micro-organisms such ag sigs, Slimes. fungi, bacteria,
Enails, and miscellaneous larvac. Thoso problems are difficult to
cvaluate ‘and prevent since thoy are affected by a number of factors,
Such problems occur whon the water contains organics and iron of
hydrogen sulphide, Ono Of the most Severe forms of ¢l0gging is caused
by a white, gelatinous sulphur slime associated with sulphur bacteria.
Another common one 1a tho brown slime mans caused by filamentous iron
bacteria. These grow rapidly in water Containing as low as 0.4 me/l
i7on and are especially troublesome in water containing soluble dark,
Lannin-like organics which act as a readily available fod source fof
the bacteria. Algae and other growths can cause problems especially if
their growen rates are enhanced by excess nutrient levels (nitrogen or
phosphorous). The use Of vastewator in localized (drip) irrigation
Systems would be especially troublesome since effluents normally
Contain nutrients, dissolved organics, and micro-organisms, all of
Which may increase the potential For clogcing problems.

Chemical treatment (chlorine) is ono of the most effective
methods for controlling biological growths but is costly and requires

5.9 VECTOR PROBLEMS ASSOCIATED WITH WATER QUALITY

In most countries where there is a risk to health from vector-

onchocerciasis, schistosomiasis, there is an awareness of the possi:

The existence of an aquatic enviroment is usually the primary

Rothods. However, there ‘ls less information on the relationships
between ‘water quality and vector production although quality aspects
may often intensify a vector problem and may even create the physical
Conditions leading to the problem.

Put simply, the ideal conditions for good irrigation management
are similar 0 hose which will discourage vector production in
Irrigated agriculture or will at least assist in vector control. This
implies a minimus of unnecessary water Surface, well constructed and
maintainod Supply Channels, Offective, unimpeded drainage of excess
Vater and efficient, economical water Application. When the quality of

increased risk of vector production.

6.3 TOXIC SUBSTANCES IN LIVESTOCK WATER
There are a number of substances or toxic lons which cause
toxicity in animals. These sometines occur naturally in water, but mors
Eroguently they are a result of man's activities, including waste
aisposal. ‘Towle substances in natural water are usually at concen
trations well below the toxic levels. Tf unusually high and toxic
levels ars found, this often implies the existence of Some outside
contaninating source such a a wastewater and the use of the water
Should be restricted until the source of the toxic clement is located
nd reduced or oliminated. The common toxicants include many inorganic
elements, organic wastes, pathogenic organisms, and herbicides and
besticides ana their residues. These may be directly toxic to che
Animal, cause the water to be unpalatabls, or accumulate in the animal
making 15s edible product unsafe or UnElt for human consumption.
he National Academy of Sciences (1972 and 1974) has prepare
Guidelines on the sate lovel of many toxic inorganic elements in
Tivestock drinking water. These are presented in Table 30. These guide
Lines have a wide saety margin. They are based on amounts normally
found in usable surface and groundwater and are not necessarily the
Timits of animal tolerance. This approach is taken since the safs
concentration of these substances is dependent upon many factors,
including the quantity of water an animal consumos each day and the
weight GF tho animal. Tho original discussions presented by the
National Academy of Sciences publication and other sources should be
consulted before using a water of questicnable quality.

The most common management problems are related to fluoride,
iron, nitrate, or hydrogen sulphide. Most of the fluoride problems
noted are not actually toxicity; rather, fluoride causes tooth oiling
ana bone problems. In areas where fluoride water constitutes the sole
25urce of livestock drinking water, attempts should be made to mininize
Svaporative concentration. If high fluoride water must be uted in
Certain seasons, alternating che exposure of the animal to it will be
helpful. An alternative approach would be retention of low fluoride
water for the use Of young Stock Since this is the most susceptible
2ge. especially before eruption of the permanent teeth.

Toxicity problems are amplified when the forage used is also
irrigated with the same potentially toxic water. The plants take up the
salts, thus raising the toxicity risk to the animal when both the
Zources of feed and water combine to exceed the critical levels. This
Ray also happen with an element such as seleniun.

Livestock poisoning by nitrates or nitrites should not occur
with levels less than the guideline values. This does not excluse all
problems, however, as a high nitrate level may cause heavy growth of
Sigas in’ watering points. No direct Link has bean established between
Reavy algae grouths and livestock deaths. Researchers point to the
possibility that the sudden decomposition Gf 4ijas may produce circu
Stances conducive to the development of botulisa. Blue-green algae have
also been suggested as containing possible toxins although no concrete
Svidence is available at present. Care should be taken when animals are
Uaing watering points with heavy growths. Copper sulphate is effective
in controlling algae growens even at concentrations of 1 mg/l but care
and professional 8dvice should be sought before using it, as the
Solution to one problen could be the start of another.

Not all unusual constituents in animal drinking water are toxic.
Some only cause management problems of nuisances. For example, a common
roblen ‘in using shallow Grouncuater ia the high level of hydrogen
Sulphide. Although by itself it does not harm the animal, che odour
Theluonces the animals to reject the water. A common practice of first
running such water don a Splash board for aeration has proved very
eifective, because the Greater part of the hydrogen Sulphide Ls dissi-
Dated before entering the tater trough or tank. Mater Containing
Bydrogen sulphide 8180 presents a corrosion problen to watering tanks
OF equipnent due to the formation of sulphuric acid.

No limits for iron are given in Table 30 because Lt has a low
order of toxicity. At watering points, iron is rarely present in the
Vater aince, on contact with air, the ferrous salts aro oxidized and
They precipitate, rendering them essentislly harmless to animals.
However, even With a few milligrams per litre, iron Can cause clogging
OF linet to Watering troughs cf an undesirable staining or deposit.

Effluent irrigation may also lead to microbial contamination of
air, soils and plants in the vicinity of tho irrigation site. The
Sxtént of such contamination depends upon the dogroe of treatment
provided, the prevailing climatic conditions, nature of the crop being
Drrigated and the Gesign Of the irrigation system. Where the verrain
and the crop type are suitable, effluents may be applied through ‘ridge
and furrow systems. These contaminate neither the alr nor the upper
parts of plants. Subsurace tile of trickle Irrigation systems create
tho fowest hazards of any Kind. Hoevor, tho expense of utilizing such
syateas on a large scale severely limite their feasibility. An addi-
tional problem is the clogging of dripper nozzles and subsurface
Pipelines due to suspondad sediments and microbial growth. Sprinklers
Croats the greatest potential for microbial contamination of the
Vegetation ana air.

When considering the use of effluents for irrigation, their
microbial and biochemical properties will have to be cvalusted. These
Values should then be compared with the public health standards, taking
{nto consideration the crop, soil and irrigation systea and consumption
of the produce, and only when tho effluent moots these standards should
Tt be evaluated in terms of chemical criteria such as dissolved salts,
relative sodium content and specific toxic ions.

In quantitative terms, the volume of wastewater available for
re-use by irrigated agriculture is negligible when compared with the
Guerall volume of water used for irrigation. However, the potential
Tmpacts ‘aesociated with water quality and agricultural re-use of
wastewater are so. important, economically, environmentally asd so
Cally, that the need for sound planning far oxceeds the relatively
Saal Guantit os and areas involved. Several sxamplos of wastewater ro
use are given in Section 8.

The following list of references contains research as well as
practical information on various aspects of the re-use of O(fluents for
rap production: Eckenfelder (1980): Loche (1977); National ‘Research
Council Of Canada (1974); Sopper and Kardos (1973); and Wilson and
Beckett (1968).

8.8 NEED FOR DRAINAGE - Tigris-Euphrates Kiver Basin, Iraq

The Tigris-Euphrates River Plain is an area that suffers with
both salinity’and high water tables. This is one of the oldest known
irrigated arcas of the world. River water salinity for most of the
irrigated area is low (EC + 0.30.7 dim); however, salinity seill
becane a problem. Records indicate that salinity problems were present
In some areas by 2400 BC and farmers wore turning from whoat to barley
because barley was a more salt tolerant crop. In other areas of tho
Plain, salinity problems were delayed until avout 100 BC (Jacobsen and
Adams 1958) Barly irrigators apparently understood the advantages of
Irrigation but did not understand tho neve For aroawide drainage.

Most of the Tigri-Euphrates Plain today is severely troubled
with both salinity and high water tables. Since the natural water
Guality of both tho Tigris and the Euphrates has boon excellent,
Salinity Should normally not be a problem (ses water analyses nos. 164
and 166 in Amnox 1). Hovover, with inadequate drainage and the result-
ing nigh water tables that developed, there was no way to control and
permanently loach any significant portion of the salts being applied in
Pho Lrrigation water. Salts Slowly eccumulated and productivity
declined. Drainage and reclamation projects are now boing implemented
and the area will no doubt again become & very productive agricultural
Grea (bicleman 1363).

8.14 KIVER WATER QUALITY VARIATIONS = Ethiopia and Somalia

Rivor water quality is often inversely related to flow; dilution
Gue to runoff in the rainy or snowmelt periods usually kesps total salt
Concentration low. A unique exception is the Wadi Shebells that

Plateau of Ethiopia and Somalia and discharges into the Indian Ocoan:

During a greater part of the year the river flow originates
mostly from the upper highlands of Ethiopia, which aro a basalt forma.
tion. River water salinity from runoff originating in this wppor
catchment area rarely exceeds ECw = 0.75 dS/m and Ls ofton well below
ECw = 0.50 GS/m (see ‘no. 158, Amex I). With good management, such
wator presents fou problems.

The river quality changes significantly in the periods from late
Apeil until early June and again in October and November (sce nos. 159
and 160, Annex I). During these periods, rivor water salinity rangos
fron ECw = 0.73-2.0 ds/m and occasionally ECw exceeds 2.5 a3/m. the
increased salinity in the Wadi Shebelle Curing this fine is associated
with high intensity rains that cause runoff from the Ogadan plateau
Unich consists of rock formations Of marine origin. The Infraguent,
high intensity rains on tho Ogadan plateau food tho Wadi Shebolle for
periods which last up to two weeks following each heavy rain. The water
Characteristics show a relatively high concentration of gypsum (Caso)
Foflecting the rock formations oh the plateau. As cropping takes place
on a year-round basis In the Fiver basin, carcful management of the
high salinity water is needed to ensure continued 900d rep Productions
Because most irrigation practices using Wadi Shebelle water are poor
and spate Irrigation is widely practised, & comson managment step is
£0 avoid using the Wai water for several days following increased
Fiver flown caused by rainfall in the ogadan plateau (Ochtman 1575):
