5.22 WASTEMATER IRRIGATION - Bakersticld, California, USA
The City of Bakersfield, located in the southern end of the San
Joaquin Valley, has uscd troatod wastewater to irrigate cropland for
more than 65 years. Normal annual raiofall Ls 150 ma and occurs mostly
© the winter months of becombor to the end of Februsry. Because of tho
mild climate, (rrigation can be practised all the yoar round.

--- PÁGINA 155 ---
Sa

The present treatment system provides primary treatment followed
by aerated deep lagoons (2. ha) and storage reservoirs which can
provide up to 30 days Of storage, if neaded, Treatment in combination
with lagoons is equivalent to secondary treatment. The treated waste.
Vater analysis Ls listed an no. 346 in Annex I.

The treated wastewater is used to irrigate approximately 2250
hectares of city-owned 1and. The city leases the land to one farmer and
the lease sots very specific requirements on Cropping patterns. The
present. crops include barley, maize, slfalfa, sorghum, and permanent
pasture. Over half of the city farm land is high in salinity and
Sodicity. The city, through the terms of its lease, encouraged tne
farmer to imploment 3 land reclamation programme that consisted of
ripping to a depth of 0.8 m, followed by land grading to pormit flood
or furrow irrigation. For reclamation, 3 pra-plant leaching irrigation
was given, followed later by 20 metric tons of 60 percent pure gypsum
per hectare, Gisked into the upper 15 cm Of the soil. Barley was then
Planted in the autumn of the first yoar and heavily irrigated in tne
winter and spring to accomplish leaching. Following tha barley, a
Sumor crop Of Sudan grass Or grain sorghus was planted and irrigated
by border check, In late summer, the field was planted to pasture or
alfalfa, and flood irrigated. Soil conditions were monitored until
Salinity levels reached 4 lovel safe enough to grew other crops under
Farrow irrigation.

In areas of nigher salinity whers additional leaching was
necessary, the farmer planted rice as a reclamation crop. The goal vas
0 alos ‘the large quantities of water needed for rice to leach ine
igh level of saits from the soil. While this practice was of fective,
the use of ‘the treated wastewater in flooded rice fields created an
abnormally high mosquito problem. The oxact feasons are unknown but
£1014 studies’ and observations by vector biologists clearly shoved
significantly nigher voceor population in the rice fields receiving
Greated wastewater ab Bakerstiold and other sites using treated
wastewater to irrigate paddy rice. Preliminary data Snow that the
RORQUItoss arc attracted fo standing water containing hign levels of
Grganice. Because of the contern for serious disease problems in tne
383acent urban population, the use of treated wastewater for tha
Ireigation of paddy rico has boon halted in California. On other Crops
the treated wastoater doss not creats vector problems ss the fields
are not cont lnuously flooded and water does not pond for long enough to
3llow mosquitoes to propagate.

The amount of nitrogen in the trated wastewster is about 250
kg/ha per metre of water appl cd. This amount will satisfy tho nitrogen
girtilizer requirements Of most crops. In ine past, probloms have
occurred with certain orops much as cotion Owing to oxcoasivo vegota-
Five growth. This was probably due to tho proscace of excess availiable
nitrogen in the irrigation water during the latter part of the growing
Season. To correct this problem; the farmer now uses the treated
wastewater, with its benoficial nitrogen, Suring the sarly part of tho
Soason, and switches to low nitrogen well or Canal wator in the later
part of the Season or blends the treated wastewater with these alter.
nate supplien to reduce the nitrogen content (Crites 1974; State Wator
Resources Control Board 1981; and EPA 1979).

8.25 USE OF WASTEWATER WIGH IN BORON - Calistoga, California, USA

California suffered two years of severe drought during the
winters of 1975-76 and 1976-77. Calistoges a small community about 100
Kn nortn of San Francisco and in the northern part of the Napa Valley
could no longer supply water to its golf course. Without water the golf
Groans and fairways wers drying up and becoming unplayable.

Municipal wastewater was available but had to be piped about 3
kn to a holding pond at the OLE course before being pub ehrough the
sprinkler system. Furthermore, the Calistoga mineral batms and spas use
Rot, mineral spring waters in their suimaing pools and mud bathe, and
the springs flow more or less continually, discharging to the treatment
plant: These mineral springs are high in boron and when mixed with tne
Tou boron domestic supply, produce a wastewater containing about § ma/1
boron. It was therefors suspected that boron could be 4 problem Lf this
water were used on the Golf course.

At the beginning of the testing period to use the municipal
wastewater in the holding pond at the Calistoga golf course it had a
Salinity (Ek) Of 1.0 ds/m, boron at 3.8 ma/1 and SAR = 3.5. During the
Two years of monitoring, boron ranged in the applied water from 3:0. to
7.8 ‘mg/l. Boron in the root sone (saturation extract basis) of the
greens ranged from 3.1 to 7.8 mg/l, and boron in the grass clippings
Tron tho greens (dry weight basis) ranged from 18 to 46 m/kg. frequent
cutting apparently prevented any damaging accumulation of boron.

The golf greens and fairways were maintained by using the
municipal wastewater for irrigation without any apparent damage from
its high boron content. Conifer trecs, however, in scactersa plantings
around” the course showed appreciable leaf namage (Lip burm). Aith the
return of nomal rainfall (500-300 ma/year) any potential damage due to
boron has ‘been kept to a minimum. Calistoga has continued Eo use the
wastewater on the GOLf course (Donaldson et al. 1978).
